data-testid="mobile-leaderboard"><div class="css-11egx89"><button class="icon-hl-close-thick css-45dtc3"></button><div class="css-1fteite" data-empty="true"><div class="css-1hjaa0k"><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-bcus5s" data-ad="true" data-adbridg-ad-class="mlb1" id="mlb1__slot"></div></div></div></aside><div data-placement="subheader"></div><div class="css-m260rd"><div class="css-13sft9o" data-article-body="0"><div class="css-z468a2"><h1>Understanding Crohn’s Disease</h1><div data-preamp="social-proof-lessons"></div><div data-testid="byline"><section class="css-jy1umg" data-testid="byline"><div class="css-1q67gt9"><div class="css-quwpxp"><img alt="" src="//i0.wp.com/post.healthline.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/01/Cynthia-Chavoustie-500x500-Bio.png?w=105&amp;h=105"/></div></div><div><span><span><a class="css-3v84bb" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Medically reviewed</a> by <a class="css-3v84bb" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Cynthia Taylor Chavoustie, MPAS, PA-C</a></span></span> — <span><span><span><span>By <a class="css-3v84bb" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Kimberly Holland</a></span></span></span></span> — <a class="css-3v84bb" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank"><span> Updated on October 5, 2023</span></a></div></section></div><div><nav class="css-7u7i0a" data-testid="tab-bar" id="tab-bar-0"><ul class="css-17ztj4b"><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#symptoms">Symptoms</a></li><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#types">Types</a></li><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#causes">Causes</a></li><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#diagnosis">Diagnosis</a></li><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#treatment">Treatment</a></li><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#faq">FAQ</a></li><li class="css-t753mo"><a class="css-ubd94x" href="#outlook">Outlook</a></li></ul></nav><article class="article-body css-d2znx6 undefined"><div class="css-1u22pos"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><p class="css-1rnzyga">Crohn’s disease affects your GI tract, leading to symptoms such as diarrhea and bloody stool. Dietary changes and medications can help you manage some symptoms. However, most people will eventually need surgery.</p></div><div class="wp-block-healthline-jwplayer"><div class="css-1ymuq24"><div class="css-oup8q3"><svg class="css-xcxd8t" viewbox="0 0 16 9"></svg></div></div></div><hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity"/><p>Crohn’s disease is a type of inflammatory bowel disease (IBD). As many as <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.crohnscolitisfoundation.org/sites/default/files/2019-02/Updated%20IBD%20Factbook.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">780,000 Americans</a> have the condition, according to the Crohn’s &amp; Colitis Foundation of America (CCFA).</p><div data-preamp="serena-chatbot"></div><p>More research on <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" data-id="https://www.healthline.com/health/prognosis-of-crohn-disease" data-type="URL" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/prognosis-of-crohn-disease">Crohn’s disease</a> is necessary. Researchers aren’t sure how it begins, who is most likely to develop it, or how to best manage it. Despite major treatment advances in the last three decades, no cure is available yet.</p><p>Crohn’s disease most commonly occurs in the small intestine and the colon. It can affect any part of your <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/human-body-maps/digestive-system">gastrointestinal (GI) tract</a>, from the mouth to the anus. It can involve some parts of the GI tract and skip other parts.</p><p>The range of severity for Crohn’s is mild to debilitating. Symptoms vary and can change over time. In severe cases, the disease can lead to life threatening flares and complications. </p><p>Here’s everything you need to know about Crohn’s disease.</p><div class="css-20w1gi"><aside class="css-0 css-0" data-sticky="false" data-testid="sticky-inline-ad"><div><div class="css-1sq15os" data-empty="true"><div class="css-1hjaa0k"><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-1t05dkn" data-ad="true" data-adbridg-ad-class="inline2__slot" id="inline2__slot"></div></div></div></aside></div></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="symptoms">Crohn’s disease symptoms</a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><p>The symptoms of Crohn’s disease often develop gradually. Certain symptoms may also become worse over time. Although it’s possible, it’s <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.aafp.org/pubs/afp/issues/2018/1201/p661-s1.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">rare</a> for symptoms to develop suddenly and dramatically. </p><p>The earliest symptoms of Crohn’s disease can include:</p><ul><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/diarrhea">diarrhea</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/abdominal-pain">abdominal cramps</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/blood-clot-in-stool">blood in your stool</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/how-to-tell-if-you-have-a-fever">fever</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/fatigue">fatigue</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/fatigue-and-loss-of-appetite">loss of appetite</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/unexplained-weight-loss">unexplained weight loss</a></li><li>feeling as if your bowels aren’t empty after a bowel movement</li><li>feeling a <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/pooping-a-lot">frequent need for bowel movements</a></li></ul><p>It’s sometimes possible to mistake these symptoms for those of another condition, such as <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/food-poisoning">food poisoning</a>, an <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/best-foods-for-upset-stomach/">upset stomach</a>, or an allergy. You should see your doctor if any of these symptoms persist.</p><p>The symptoms may become more severe as the disease progresses. More serious symptoms may include:</p><ul><li>a <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/anorectal-abscess">perianal fistula</a>, which causes pain and drainage near your anus</li><li>ulcers that may occur anywhere from the mouth to the anus</li><li>inflammation of the joints and skin</li><li>shortness of breath or decreased ability to exercise due to anemia</li></ul><p>Early detection and diagnosis can help you avoid severe complications and allow you to begin treatment early. </p><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="types">What are the variations of Crohn’s disease?</a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><p>There are five variations of Crohn’s disease, all based on location in the digestive system. They are:</p><ul><li><strong>Gastroduodenal Crohn’s disease: </strong><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/gastroduodenal-crohns-disease">This type</a> affects the stomach and the duodenum, which is the first part of your small intestine. </li><li><strong>Jejunoileitis: </strong>This type affects the second portion of your intestine, called the jejunum.</li><li><strong>Ileitis:</strong> This type affects the ileum, which is the last part of the small intestine.</li><li><strong>Ileocolitis:</strong> This type affects the ileum and the colon.</li><li><strong>Crohn’s colitis:</strong> This type affects the colon only. </li></ul><p>In addition, Crohn’s disease can present as <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/perianal-crohns-disease">perianal disease</a>, which involves fistulas (abnormal connections between tissues), deep tissue infections, as well as sores and ulcers on the outer skin around the anus.</p><div class="css-20w1gi"><aside class="css-0 css-0" data-sticky="false" data-testid="sticky-inline-ad"><div><div class="css-1sq15os" data-empty="true"><div class="css-1hjaa0k"><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-1t05dkn" data-ad="true" data-adbridg-ad-class="inline7__slot" id="inline7__slot"></div></div></div></aside></div></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="causes">What causes Crohn’s disease?</a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><p>It isn’t clear what causes Crohn’s disease. </p><p>One possibility is that it’s a type of <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/autoimmune-disorders">autoimmune condition</a>. This is a type of condition in which <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/autoimmune-disorders/">your immune system mistakenly attacks</a> your body, sometimes causing inflammation. </p><p>Though the immune system could be a potential contributor to inflammation in Crohn’s, it has not yet been classified as <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/autoimmune-disorders/">an autoimmune condition</a> as the causes are still not fully understood.</p><h3>Risk factors</h3><p>The following factors may influence your chances of developing it:</p><ul><li>inheriting certain genes</li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.crohnscolitisfoundation.org/what-is-crohns-disease/causes" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">having relatives</a> with the disease, and in particular, <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.aafp.org/pubs/afp/issues/2018/1201/p661.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">having a twin</a> with the disease</li><li>smoking</li><li>using medications like <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/birth-control-effects-on-body/">oral contraceptives</a>, antibiotics, and nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)</li><li>living in an urban environment</li></ul><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="diagnosis">Crohn’s disease diagnosis</a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><p><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/doctor-diagnosis">Crohn’s is typically diagnosed</a> based on the results of tissue samples taken from your digestive tract during an upper or lower <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/endoscopy/">endoscopy</a>. </p><p>Other <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/tests">tests</a> may also point to Crohn’s disease, and their results — combined with your symptoms and history — will be taken into consideration. These tests <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK436021/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">include</a></hl-trusted-source> blood and <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/stool-culture/">stool tests</a>, as well as an <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/endoscopy">endoscopy</a> and <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/6-tips-for-making-colonoscopy-prep-more-tolerable">colonoscopy</a>, during which your doctor will likely do a <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/biopsy">biopsy</a>. You may also need imaging tests like <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/ct-scan">CT</a> and <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/abdominal-mri-scan">MRI</a>.</p><p>Once the doctor reviews the results of all the tests and gets a clear picture of your symptoms, they may conclude you have Crohn’s disease. </p><p>Your doctor may go on to request these tests several more times to look for affected tissue and determine how the disease is progressing. </p><p>The <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://care.healthline.com/find-care" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Healthline FindCare tool</a> can provide options in your area if you need help finding a <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/find-care/articles/primary-care-doctors/types-of-doctors/">primary care doctor</a> or a gastroenterologist.</p><figure class="css-oei5ji"></figure><div class="css-20w1gi"><aside class="css-0 css-0" data-sticky="false" data-testid="sticky-inline-ad"><div><div class="css-1sq15os" data-empty="true"><div class="css-1hjaa0k"><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-1t05dkn" data-ad="true" data-adbridg-ad-class="inline12__slot" id="inline12__slot"></div></div></div></aside></div></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="treatment">Treatment for Crohn’s disease</a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><p>A cure for Crohn’s disease isn’t available yet, but the disease can be managed. A variety of treatment options exist that can lessen the severity and frequency of your symptoms.</p><h3>Medications</h3><p>Several types of medications are available to treat Crohn’s. These include:</p><ul><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/anti-diarrheal-drugs">antidiarrheals</a></li><li>anti-inflammatory drugs</li><li>biologics</li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/immune-system-suppressors">immunomodulators</a></li></ul><p><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/antibiotics">Antibiotics</a> may help reduce some of the symptoms of Crohn’s and some of the possible triggers for it. For example, antibiotics <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5198245/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">can reduce</a></hl-trusted-source> drainage and heal <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/gastrointestinal-fistula">fistulas</a>, which are abnormal connections between tissues that Crohn’s can cause. </p><h3>Surgery</h3><p>If less invasive treatments and lifestyle changes don’t improve your symptoms, <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/surgery">surgery</a> may be necessary. Ultimately, <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://crohnsandcolitis.ca/About-Crohn-s-Colitis/IBD-Journey/Treatment-and-Medications/Surgery" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">about 80%</a> of people with Crohn’s disease will require surgery at some point in their lives, according to the CCFA.</p><p>Types of surgeries include:</p><ul><li>strictureplasty</li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/small-bowel-resection">bowel resection</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/colostomy">ostomy</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/surgery-colectomies">colectomy</a></li><li><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/total-proctocolectomy-with-ileostomy">proctocolectomy</a></li></ul><p>Crohn’s disease surgery is helpful for relieving symptoms, but it’s not without risk. Talk with your doctor about any concerns you may have with surgery.</p><h3>Diet</h3><p>Food doesn’t cause Crohn’s disease, but it can trigger flares. A diet plan that works for one person with Crohn’s disease may not work for another. This is because the disease can involve different areas of the GI tract in different people.</p><p>It’s important to find out what works best for you. This can be done by keeping track of your symptoms as you add or remove certain foods from your diet and in consultation with your doctor or a registered dietitian. </p><p>Lifestyle and diet changes that may help you include:</p><ul><li><strong>Reduce fiber:</strong> For some people, foods with fiber may aggravate the GI tract. If this is true for you, you may need to switch to a <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/low-residue-diet">low residue diet</a>. <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK557693/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Research</a></hl-trusted-source> on this particular diet has been mixed, so speak with your doctor about your personal needs.</li><li><strong>Limit fat:</strong> Crohn’s disease may interfere with your body’s ability to break down and absorb fat. However, a <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2017/06/170622121911.htm" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">2017 study</a> on mice suggested that a diet higher in plant-based fats may positively affect the <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/gut-microbiome-and-health/">gut microbiome</a> in people with Crohn’s disease. More research is needed, and your doctor or a registered dietitian can help guide you in your fat intake.</li><li><strong>Limit dairy: </strong>Even if you’re not <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/lactose-intolerance-101">lactose intolerant</a>, your body <hl-trusted-source rationale="Peer reviewed journal" source="BMC/BioMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://nutritionj.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12937-016-0183-8" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">can develop difficulty</a></hl-trusted-source> digesting some dairy products when you have Crohn’s disease. Consuming dairy can lead to an upset stomach, abdominal cramps, and diarrhea for some people.</li><li><strong>Drink water:</strong> Crohn’s disease may affect your body’s ability to absorb water from your digestive tract. This can lead to <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.crohnsandcolitis.org.uk/about-crohns-and-colitis/publications/dehydration" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">dehydration</a>. The risk of <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/dehydration/">dehydration</a> is especially high if you’re having diarrhea or bleeding.</li><li><strong>Take vitamin and mineral supplements: </strong>Crohn’s disease can affect your intestines’ ability to properly absorb other nutrients from your food. Eating <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/11-most-nutrient-dense-foods-on-the-planet">nutrient-dense foods</a> may not be enough. Talk with your doctor about taking <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/best-multivitamins-for-men/">multivitamins</a> to find out if this is right for you.</li></ul><h3>Natural treatments </h3><p>Some people use <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/alternative-treatments">complementary and alternative medicine (CAM)</a> to help manage symptoms of various conditions and diseases, including Crohn’s disease. </p><p>The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) hasn’t approved these for treatment, but some people use them in addition to mainstream medications.</p><p>Don’t add any new treatments to your current treatment plan without consulting your doctor.</p><p>Some CAM treatments for Crohn’s disease include:</p><ul><li><strong>Probiotics:</strong> <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/probiotics-101">These</a> may help prevent microorganisms from upsetting your gut’s natural balance and causing a Crohn’s flare. Scientific data about effectiveness is limited. </li><li><strong>Prebiotics</strong>: These are <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/probiotics-and-prebiotics">potentially beneficial materials</a> found in plants that help feed the good bacteria in your gut and increase their numbers. </li><li><strong>Fish oil: </strong>Oily fish like salmon and mackerel are rich in <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/nutrition/13-benefits-of-fish-oil">omega-3s</a>. <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://karger.com/mpp/article/26/3/201/204278/Antioxidant-Supplements-and-Gastrointestinal" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Research</a> is ongoing regarding the use of <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/fish-oil-for-hair/">fish oil</a> in the treatment of Crohn’s disease.</li><li><strong>Supplements:</strong> Some herbs, vitamins, and minerals may help ease the symptoms of Crohn’s disease. <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8998137/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Research</a></hl-trusted-source> is ongoing as to which supplements may be beneficial.</li><li><strong>Acupuncture:</strong> <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC9280737/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Research</a></hl-trusted-source> has found that <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/complementary-therapy#acupuncture">acupuncture</a>, combined with <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/moxibustion">moxibustion</a> — a type of traditional Chinese medicine that involves burning dried medicinal herbs on or near your skin — may help improve some symptoms. </li></ul><p>Tell your doctor if you use any CAM treatments or over-the-counter medications. Some of these substances can affect the efficiency of medications or other treatments. In some cases, an interaction or side effect could be dangerous or even life threatening.</p><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="faq">Frequently asked questions </a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><h3>What is the life expectancy of someone with Crohn’s disease?</h3><p>According to a <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7669301/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Canadian study from 2020</a></hl-trusted-source>, the average life expectancy for women with IBD was 78.4 years, and the average life expectancy for men with IBD was 75.5 years.</p><h3>Can children develop Crohn’s disease?</h3><p>Most people with Crohn’s disease receive a diagnosis in their 20s and 30s, but <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/inflammatory-bowel-disease">IBD</a> can develop in children, too. Approximately <hl-trusted-source rationale="Highly respected database from the National Institutes of Health" source="PubMed Central"><a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6034144/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">1 in 4 people</a></hl-trusted-source> with an IBD show symptoms before age 18. </p><p>Untreated Crohn’s in children can lead to <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5 sl" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/delayed-growth-symptom/">growth delays</a> and weakened bones. However, Crohn’s medications can have some significant side effects on children. It’s vital you work closely with your child’s doctor to find the right options.</p><h3>What is the difference between Crohn’s disease and ulcerative colitis?</h3><p>Crohn’s disease and <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/ulcerative-colitis">ulcerative colitis (UC)</a> are two types of IBD. They have many of the same characteristics. You may even mistake them for one another. </p><p>That said, there are also some differences. <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://www.healthline.com/health/crohns-disease/crohns-ibd-uc-difference">Learn more about Crohn’s disease versus ulcerative colitis.</a></p><script type="application/ld+json">

</script></div><div class="css-0"><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><div><h2><div><a class="chartbeat-section" name="outlook">Outlook</a></div></h2></div><span style="font-size:0;line-height:0"></span><p>Research is still ongoing to find more effective treatments and potentially an eventual cure for Crohn’s disease. But symptoms can be successfully managed, and remission is possible. </p><p>Your doctor can help guide you in finding the right medications, alternative treatments, and lifestyle measures that can help. </p><p>If you’re having gastrointestinal symptoms, speak with your doctor to determine the cause and potential solutions.</p><p>Meeting others within the Crohn’s community can be extremely helpful. <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://go.onelink.me/LOC7/21953a3a" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">IBD Healthline</a> is a free app that connects you with others who understand what you’re going through via one-on-one messaging, live group discussions, and expert-approved information on managing IBD. </p><p>Download the app for <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://go.onelink.me/LOC7/21953a3a" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">iPhone</a> or <a class="content-link css-1xhnmo5" href="https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.healthline.ibd" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Android</a>.</p></div></article><span class="css-zya54r"></span></div><p class="css-1kir5of"><span> Last medically reviewed on  October 5, 2023</span></p><div><div class="css-1bn2hgp"><div class="css-1rqcb0a"><h3 id="bylineArticleTools">How we reviewed this The range of severity for Crohn’s disease is mild to debilitating. In severe cases, the disease can lead to life threatening flares and complications. Symptoms vary and can change over time. They can include: diarrhea, abdominal cramps, blood in your stool, fatigue, loss of appetite. Most people with Crohn’s disease receive a diagnosis in their 20’s and 30’s, but IBD can develop in children, too. A cure for Crohn’s disease isn’t available yet, but the disease can be managed through medications, alternative treatments, dietary changes and lifestyle measures."]]}}]},{"tagName":"hr","attributes":{"className":"wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity"}},{"tagName":"p","source":"Crohn’s disease is a type of inflammatory bowel disease (IBD).
